  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app',

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer8382640528542588096">Considered by critics to be Barth's most distinguished masterpiece, <i>The Sot-Weed Factor</i> has acquired the status of a modern classic. Set in the late 1600s, it recounts the wildly chaotic odyssey of hapless, ungainly Ebenezer Cooke, sent to the New World to look after his father's tobacco business and to record the struggles of the Maryland colony in an epic poem.<br/><br/>On his mis</span>
  <span id="freeText8382640528542588096" style="display:none">Considered by critics to be Barth's most distinguished masterpiece, <i>The Sot-Weed Factor</i> has acquired the status of a modern classic. Set in the late 1600s, it recounts the wildly chaotic odyssey of hapless, ungainly Ebenezer Cooke, sent to the New World to look after his father's tobacco business and to record the struggles of the Maryland colony in an epic poem.<br /><br />On his mission, Cooke experiences capture by pirates and Indians; the loss of his father's estate to roguish impostors; love for a farmer prostitute; stealthy efforts to rob him of his virginity, which he is (almost) determined to protect; and an extraordinary gallery of treacherous characters who continually switch identities. A hilarious, bawdy tribute to all the most insidious human vices, <i>The Sot-Weed Factor</i> has a lasting relevance for readers of all times.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8382640528542588096" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">This or Lost in the Funhouse. LITF isn't all golden, but it's a quicker way to get a taste of his style if you're hesitant to read this beast of a boo<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">This or Lost in the Funhouse. LITF isn't all golden, but it's a quicker way to get a taste of his style if you're hesitant to read this beast of a book. Some of its stories are absolute classics, too.<br /><br />I would only recommend the Floating Opera or the End of the Road if you're a completist. Both are about as average as you can get, IMO.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/65909643-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1515603070p2/65909643.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2110656283?book_show_action=true">Sep 01, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/65909643-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2110656283" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14329950118484105907">This book is a sheer marvel. Set in the 1600s, it's awash in lyrical excess, bawdy humor, historical satire, human vice, roguish fools, epic intent, and pirates and Indians and prostitutes and poets, oh my! The sheer life force of this novel is amazing, the prose is masterful and wickedly funny, and the journey is like nothing I've ever been on before. Now I'll shut up and let the far more eloquent Mr. Barth take over. Here's the opening line:<br/><br/>"In the last years of the Seventeenth Century there w</span>
  <span id="freeText14329950118484105907" style="display:none">This book is a sheer marvel. Set in the 1600s, it's awash in lyrical excess, bawdy humor, historical satire, human vice, roguish fools, epic intent, and pirates and Indians and prostitutes and poets, oh my! The sheer life force of this novel is amazing, the prose is masterful and wickedly funny, and the journey is like nothing I've ever been on before. Now I'll shut up and let the far more eloquent Mr. Barth take over. Here's the opening line:<br /><br />"In the last years of the Seventeenth Century there was to be found among the fops and fools of the London coffee-houses one rangy, gangling flitch called Ebenezer Cooke, more ambitious than talented, and yet more talented than prudent, who, like his friends-in-folly, all of whom were supposed to be educating at Oxford or Cambridge, had found the sound of Mother English more fun to game with than her sense to labor over, and so rather than applying himself to the pains of scholarship, had learned the knack of versifying, and ground out quires of couplets after the fashion of the day, afroth with Joves and Jupiters, aclang with jarring rhymes, and string-taut with similes stretched to the snapping-point."</span>
  <a data-text-id="14329950118484105907" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1321544367" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1321544367" />
    <a title="Fionnuala" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5498525-fionnuala"><img alt="Fionnuala" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1368182051p2/5498525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1321544367?book_show_action=true">Jun 30, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fionnuala" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fionnuala" href="/user/show/5498525-fionnuala">Fionnuala</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780385240888" href="/book/show/469361.The_Sot_Weed_Factor">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=metamorphosis">metamorphosis</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1321544367" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12761704157737317144"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1438002057i/15654853.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>Escher: Sphere, 1942, Maple, diameter 235mm<br/><br/>I visited the Escher museum in The Hague recently. It was a real treat because I've been fascinated by Escher's work since I first saw his ‘Hand Drawing Itself’ <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1438081411i/15662974._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
 <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> and some of his other drawing puzzles when I was a teenager. I often doodle my own versions of puzzle drawings, turning a person's profile into an architectural feature, for example, or making a geometric pattern reveal a face hidden inside it. There's an absu</span>
  <span id="freeText12761704157737317144" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1438002057i/15654853.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Escher: Sphere, 1942, Maple, diameter 235mm<br><br>I visited the Escher museum in The Hague recently. It was a real treat because I've been fascinated by Escher's work since I first saw his ‘Hand Drawing Itself’ <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1438081411i/15662974._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> and some of his other drawing puzzles when I was a teenager. I often doodle my own versions of puzzle drawings, turning a person's profile into an architectural feature, for example, or making a geometric pattern reveal a face hidden inside it. There's an absurdity about such puzzle drawings that I love. There's no meaning, just lines assembled in curious ways. I simply enjoy the astonishment of making a few lines amount to more than is at first apparent. <br><br>Escher seems to enjoy astonishment too. The works I saw at the museum, and in the anthology I bought afterwards, are truly astonishing, and while at first I thought they were simply complex feats of magic and illusion, little by little I began to see meaning in them. Escher, in his own way, is a spinner of story cycles even if his stories are not at first obvious. With careful observation of the different elements, the parts become relatively clear, the method is understood, and the entire work becomes accessible. This is easier when we understand the preposterous parallels of his graphic language, the way he suggests the spatial in the flat and the flat in the spatial.<br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1438081571i/15662999._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
  <br>
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1438002126i/15654855._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br>The works that most clearly demonstrate his storytelling ability are his series of Metamorphoses. I had never realised how many of these he had done. Here are just two examples:<br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1438002542i/15654907._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
  <br>
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1438081728i/15663037._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br>Like a writer, he had certain themes he reworked constantly in his series. The Angel versus Devil sphere I used at the beginning of the review illustrates one of them: the dark side of our world versus the brighter side. In one of his Metamorphoses, day becomes night as black birds turn into white ones. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1438002252i/15654870._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br>In another, pure white doves become fish which turn into great black toads which then become fierce looking birds before turning back into innocent white doves again. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1438002252i/15654869._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br>At this point you are probably thinking that I’ve mistaken the page and instead of this being a review of John Barth's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24835.The_Sot_Weed_Factor" title="The Sot-Weed Factor by John Barth" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sot-Weed Factor</a>, it is instead a review of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16283911.The_Magic_of_M_C__Escher" title="The Magic of M.C. Escher by J.L. Locher" rel="nofollow noopener">The Magic of M.C. Escher</a>. <br><br>The truth is, this digression has served to make a point: we can do what we like in a review, meta and morph it however we choose just as John Barth does in his fiction: he thumbs his nose at literary convention and revels in piling up absurd coincidences and preposterous parallels. He distorts our perceptions so that we can no longer be sure of what we see. He shifts the poles of ‘morality’ as he pleases, angels are devils and devils are angels by turns. And civilisation? Where on this earthly sphere is that to be found? Certainly not where we tend to think.<br><br>His is spectacular story-telling, designed to astonish, and not averse to far more outlandish digressions than I’ve essayed here. He uses such obscure language and phraseology that it can confuse the reader at the beginning, but it only takes a short time to adjust to it and then everything is relatively clear and transparent. <br><br>One of his favourite strategies is metamorphosis: characters are rarely what they seem. They don disguises as smoothly and as frequently as the tessellated images in this Escher print. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1438007521i/15655468._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br> <br>Sometimes, however, the metamorphosis is carried out by the trials of life itself - this is the case for women characters more frequently than for male characters. Eve is never done paying the price of seduction it seems. The main character is one of the few who manage to magically retain a dove-like innocence in spite of passing through many tribulations and transmogrifications.<br><br>The entire book can be seen as a kind of giant metamorphosis. Starting out as straight historical fiction, it seems to pass through many guises from sharp satire to outright farce. We catch glimpses of authors and works we know in its pages, from Tristram Shandy to Don Quixote, from Rabelais to Shakespeare, from Joyce to the Bible. The only thing that’s missing is a whale!<br><br>…………………………………<br><br>In spite of my little shill-I, shall-I shuffle near the mid-point of this book when I was almost taken captive by the pirate ship Morpheides, I made it, will-he, nill-he, to as neat an 'alls-well that ends-well' summing-up as ever Shakespeare summoned up, and which Barth justifies with such wonderful casuistry that I declare him Saint John the Chronicler, patron saint of story-tellers henceforth and forever.<br><br>Read it and indulge in <i>laughters low</i>. <br>Amen.</["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="12761704157737317144" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_413806240" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/413806240" />
    <a title="Richard Derus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3920693-richard-derus"><img alt="Richard Derus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1494992210p2/3920693.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/413806240?book_show_action=true">Sep 13, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard Derus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard Derus" href="/user/show/3920693-richard-derus">Richard Derus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/3246225-the-sot-weed-factor">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer413806240" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer352380389303980667"><b>Well-loved books from my past</b><br/><br/>Rating: 5 golden stars of five, with a rapturous yodel cluster <br/><br/><b>The Publisher Says</b>: Considered by critics to be Barth's most distinguished masterpiece, <i>The Sot-Weed Factor</i> has acquired the status of a modern classic. Set in the late 1600s, it recounts the wildly chaotic odyssey of hapless, ungainly Ebenezer Cooke, sent to the New World to look after his father's tobacco business and to record the struggles of the Maryland colony in an epic poem. On his mission, Cooke </span>
  <span id="freeText352380389303980667" style="display:none"><b>Well-loved books from my past</b><br><br>Rating: 5 golden stars of five, with a rapturous yodel cluster <br><br><b>The Publisher Says</b>: Considered by critics to be Barth's most distinguished masterpiece, <i>The Sot-Weed Factor</i> has acquired the status of a modern classic. Set in the late 1600s, it recounts the wildly chaotic odyssey of hapless, ungainly Ebenezer Cooke, sent to the New World to look after his father's tobacco business and to record the struggles of the Maryland colony in an epic poem. On his mission, Cooke experiences capture by pirates and Indians; the loss of his father's estate to roguish impostors; love for a farmer prostitute; stealthy efforts to rob him of his virginity, which he is (almost) determined to protect; and an extraordinary gallery of treacherous characters who continually switch identities. A hilarious, bawdy tribute to all the most insidious human vices, <i>The Sot-Weed Factor</i> has lasting relevance for readers of all times. <br><br><b>My Review</b>: The book description is a bit weak-kneed, but I can't find a better one, and I detest writing the book reports with a passion.<br><br>A couple months ago, I started a re-read of this book that did not go well. I sighed. I snorted. I rolled my eyes, and cut up rough whenever we got into the book's faux-antique Englysshe. I was responding to it like it was a phauntaiysee nawvelle with majgickq and other such borderline-criminal goins-on. I put it aside, and I forgot it, except to renew it online from the Port Washington liberry.<br><br>Damn me anyway! Why can't I listen to my REAL self?! <i>John Barth,</i> my Real Self murmured, <i>John Barth of <b>The Floating Opera</b> and this book which you adored thirty years ago, he deserves better than this,</i> to which Angry Self replied, “Shut up you! Seven hundred plus pages of this phauntaaahsticall-ness will make us homicidal! Why not encourage me to read Dickens or Tolkien if all you want to is encourage me to massacre random strangers? Silence! Begone!”<br><br>Damn me! What an ass! I read the first six chapters and tossed the book aside! But...I did keep renewing it....<br><br>And today, today with two days left on my final renewal, to-goddam-day I pick the book up again. And I read the first paragraph/line. And oh damn me! Damn me! How beautiful, how simply and completely perfect it is, and how I wish I could boil Angry Me in oil!<br><br><blockquote>In the last years of the seventeenth century there was to be found among the fops and fools of the London coffee-houses one rangy, gangling flitch called Ebenezer Cooke, more ambitious than talented, and yet more talented than prudent, who, like his friends-in-folly, all of whom were supposed to be educating at Oxford or Cambridge, had found the sound of Mother English more fun to game with than her sense to labor over, and so rather than applying himself to the pains of scholarship, had learned the knack of versifying, and ground out quires of couplets after the fashion of the day, afroth with <i>Joves</i> and <i>Jupiters</i>, aclang with jarring rhymes, and string-taut with similes stretched to the snapping-point.</blockquote><br><br>Oh. Oh oh oh oh. I just had a crisis.<br><br>Now I *could* just power through the seven hundred-plus remaining pages in the next two days, ignoring all other beings and duties...to the detriment of our carpets, as the dog would be on her own re: eliminatory functions, and the complete bumfuzzlement of my houseys as I would not be showing up at the station to fetch them...but it's not on. It's just not. This isn't a book to be <i>got through</i>, it is a book to be appreciated, savored, delighted in.<br><br>I will await the tides of fortune washing a copy of my own back up on the shores of my private liberry. It is worth the wait. The rapturous narcosis of my first immersion has returned. Thirty years are as but a moment. John Barth is still there, his words as gorgeously deployed as ever they were. <br><br>Delightful. Delightful.<br><br>Damn me anyway!<br><br><a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-sa/3.0/deed.en_US" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img alt="Creative Commons License" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380148217i/680962.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br>This work is licensed under a <a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-sa/3.0/deed.en_US" rel="nofollow noopener">Creative Commons Attribution-NonCommercial-ShareAlike 3.0 Unported License</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="352380389303980667" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_359528372" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/359528372" />
    <a title="B0nnie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7355135-b0nnie"><img alt="B0nnie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1325923651p2/7355135.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/359528372?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="B0nnie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="B0nnie" href="/user/show/7355135-b0nnie">B0nnie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780586018927" href="/book/show/1222741.The_Sot_Weed_Factor">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to B0nnie by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis">Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7355135-b0nnie?shelf=favourite-books">favourite-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer359528372" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4203563168775289600">What a fun book. I'd like to compare it to <a href='http://www.bobdylan.com/us/songs/bob-dylans-115th-dream' rel='nofollow noopener'>Bob Dylan’s 115th Dream</a>, but alas it's already been done. The song's plot is not all that far from what John Barth is up to in <i>The Sot-Weed Factor</i>, but Barth is far more (dare I say it) exhausting. <br/><br/>This is a mock history of the real life poet Ebenezer Cooke, who wrote the Hudibrastic poem <a href='http://openlibrary.org/books/OL6951509M/The_sot-weed_factor' rel='nofollow noopener'>"The sot-weed factor: or, A voyage to Maryland. A satyr. In which is describ'd the laws, government, courts and constitutions of the country, and also the buildings, f</a></span>
  <span id="freeText4203563168775289600" style="display:none">What a fun book. I'd like to compare it to <a href="http://www.bobdylan.com/us/songs/bob-dylans-115th-dream" rel="nofollow noopener">Bob Dylan’s 115th Dream</a>, but alas it's already been done. The song's plot is not all that far from what John Barth is up to in <i>The Sot-Weed Factor</i>, but Barth is far more (dare I say it) exhausting. <br><br>This is a mock history of the real life poet Ebenezer Cooke, who wrote the Hudibrastic poem <a href="http://openlibrary.org/books/OL6951509M/The_sot-weed_factor" rel="nofollow noopener">"The sot-weed factor: or, A voyage to Maryland. A satyr. In which is describ'd the laws, government, courts and constitutions of the country, and also the buildings, feasts, frolicks, entertainments and drunken humours of the inhabitants of that part of America. In burlesque verse".</a><br> <br>That title is sort of a plot outline for the novel, with the million little twists and turns left out.<br><blockquote>
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380382568i/740471._SY540_.jpg" width="300" height="400" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</blockquote>Barth's meticulous attention to historical detail and his excellent imitation of the tone and style of the 18th c. novel had me believing this world. Slightly. The plot is outrageous! There is a crazy coincidence on nearly every page. And it's so dense and complicated that you need a chart to figure it all out. <br> <br>On one level Barth is a serious storyteller, but he pushes his writing to the outer limits of the sensible - as in this delightful chapter heading, "The Poet Wonders Whether the Course of Human History Is a Progress, a Drama, a Retrogression, a Cycle, an Undulation, a Vortex, a Right- or Left-Handed Spiral, a Mere Continuum, or What Have You. Certain Evidence Is Brought Forward, but of an Ambiguous and Inconclusive Nature." <br><br>If you read this book with a straight face it's very satisfying - but a silly grin with occasional laughter works too.<br><br>Ah, except for poor Joan Toast.<br><br>It's like this <a href="http://youtu.be/oFpL-Zbbfcw" rel="nofollow noopener">song</a> - you can laugh...or just go with it.<br> <br>There are stories within stories, characters within characters, plots within plots. Books within books. <br><br>Things either go horribly wrong or wondrously right, as if there were an outside hand always intervening. <br><blockquote>
<img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380382568i/740472.jpg" width="220" height="300" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>John Barth</blockquote>From how many ships must a man get tossed,<br>Before you call him drowned??<br>Get the ebook <a href="http://archive.org/stream/sotweedfactor006326mbp#page/n5/mode/2up" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>. <br> <br></span>
  <a data-text-id="4203563168775289600" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer11708808240864734921"><i>When we heap obloquy on Satan, is't not ourselves we scold, for that we secretly admire his Heavenly insurrection?</i><br/><br/>I knew naught of John Barth till I read this. He was a Marylander by birth and here, his third novel, he writes an historical fiction of earliest Maryland. There are recognizable names, and recognizable vignettes, but Barth takes liberties. It is, instead, an historical farce, and so perforce more honest.<br/><br/>We Americans are a self-loathing lot. That Satan thing, <i>supra</i>. We take an odd pr</span>
  <span id="freeText11708808240864734921" style="display:none"><i>When we heap obloquy on Satan, is't not ourselves we scold, for that we secretly admire his Heavenly insurrection?</i><br /><br />I knew naught of John Barth till I read this. He was a Marylander by birth and here, his third novel, he writes an historical fiction of earliest Maryland. There are recognizable names, and recognizable vignettes, but Barth takes liberties. It is, instead, an historical farce, and so perforce more honest.<br /><br />We Americans are a self-loathing lot. That Satan thing, <i>supra</i>. We take an odd pride in the swamp that spawned us. And so we beshit ourselves, not always figuratively. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Notice my use of the present tense. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />Barth gets that, and he portrays it brilliantly through the language of that time. It's never false, often hilarious, and in combination, profound: <i>think not we crave a swiving pure and simple at any time as do men always.</i><br /><br />Still, there was a lot here that made me wince. I speak generally about the atrocities Barth heaps on every woman character, even as he writes them strong and wise and generous; and I speak specifically about The Rite of Holy Eggplant, which was as juvenile as it was implausible.<br /><br /><i>No man is what or whom I take him for,</i> says our protagonist. It's true of the plot, certainly, and beyond that, too, it canst be gainsaid.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_201326620" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/201326620" />
    <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye"><img alt="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594424898p2/5022264.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/201326620?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye">Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780586018927" href="/book/show/1222741.The_Sot_Weed_Factor">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=exert-yourself">exert-yourself</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=read-2015">read-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews-5-stars">reviews-5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=barth">barth</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer201326620" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15130963556623871096"><b>Sextants and Parallels</b><br/><br/>John Barth took four years to write this epic Epic, and published it at the age of 30 in 1960.<br/><br/>I more or less spent four days inside its four walls over Easter (I was determined to gobble it up before the chocolate Easter eggs were finished!), but I could spend a lifetime (or what little remains of it) recounting its marvels.<br/><br/>This was my third Barth novel. I loved the first two. But this one totally blew my mind, both in terms of ambition and execution.<br/><br/><b>Swords and Cannons</b><br/><br/>I hav</span>
  <span id="freeText15130963556623871096" style="display:none"><b>Sextants and Parallels</b><br><br>John Barth took four years to write this epic Epic, and published it at the age of 30 in 1960.<br><br>I more or less spent four days inside its four walls over Easter (I was determined to gobble it up before the chocolate Easter eggs were finished!), but I could spend a lifetime (or what little remains of it) recounting its marvels.<br><br>This was my third Barth novel. I loved the first two. But this one totally blew my mind, both in terms of ambition and execution.<br><br><b>Swords and Cannons</b><br><br>I have my favourite novelists, just as I have my favourite novels. I'm reluctant to canonise authors, let alone entire oeuvres, or even individual novels. Hence, despite my favourites, I've always been reluctant to claim that there might be such a thing as a Great American Novel (which is little more than a marketing term), let alone one whose glory extends beyond the boundaries of the United States.<br><br>Yet, having just finished this work, I'm tempted to argue that it's the best American novel written in the twentieth century. The only thing that holds me back is the fact that I haven't read Pynchon's <i>"Mason &amp; Dixon"</i>, which explores the past like this novel.<br><br><b>Art and Wile</b><br><br>One of the reasons for my enthusiasm is how the work fits into the history of the novel.<br><br>It's at once a parody and an homage. But it also passes itself off as a pretty amazing iteration of what it seeks to parody.<br><br>I read and enjoyed a lot of voluminous, early English novels in my youth, before I became more impatient with my time. Midway through my life, I briefly doubted the virtue of length and maximalism, arguing that, if a writer had 900 pages in them, then why couldn't they split them into three discrete works?<br><br>This experience has persuaded me that, at least in Barth's case, I should trust the author's assessment of appropriate length:<br><br><i>"The tale is no marvel of brevity...yet it must be told."</i><br><br>In this tale, Barth immerses us, sometimes over our heads, in both a world and a worldview, and it's a delightenment.<br><br>There were times when the pace of the novel seemed to slow, and I wondered why there were still hundreds of pages to go. However, each time, in retrospect, it seemed as if Barth was merely slowing down to take a corner. Once through it, he accelerated, and the tale was off again, even if sometimes on a different tangent.<br><br><b>Raillery and Bookish Converse</b><br><br>Barth argues that this is when he discovered what we now call Post-Modernism. He might be right, insofar as the movement embraces imitation.<br><br>He would say later that novels like <i>"The Sot-Weed Factor"</i> are <i>"novels which imitate the form of the Novel, by an author who imitates the role of Author."</i><br><br><b>Pranks and Larks</b><br><br>What I love about this assertion is the degree of mischief implicit in it.<br><br>If we have read any of these earlier novels (from the late seventeenth and early eighteenth centuries), readers will be familiar with the form adopted by their authors (especially the chapter headings that sometimes sound like head notes in reports of legal cases).<br><br>Yet we also know that how they were written and what they wrote about reflected the time in which they were written.<br><br>Barth might attempt to write in this manner, but he is/was still a twentieth century (schizoid) man writing in the form of an eighteenth century novelist.<br><br>To an extent he was a passenger in someone else's vehicle.<br><br>He might have donned the garb, and he might have looked the part, but he and we are both conscious that it's a pretence.<br><br>Aye, there's the rub!<br><br>But what a pretence!<br><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1428448768i/14425588._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Gerrit Dou imitates an ear-ringed Barth<br><br><br><b>Ink and Quill</b><br><br>How to describe this fiction then?<br><br><i>"Dear God! 'Tis marvellous. What a comedy! 'Twas a marvelous tale, well told, and as nicely pointed as one of Aesop's. A pretty tale indeed, if not a virtuous. Aye, spread the word!"</i><br><br><b>Wags and Wits</b><br><br>Barth cautions us against taking authors too seriously or at their word:<br><br><i>"'Tis a great mistake for a tale-teller to philosophise and tell us what his story means; haply it doth not mean what he thinks at all, at least to the rest of us."</i><br><br>On first appearance, this might seem to warn us against emphasising too much the author's literary or philosophical intentions.<br><br>To this extent, it urges us to enjoy the author's play (authors and characters alike can <i>"play this world like a harpsichord"),</i> not just their earnestness.<br><br><b>Spin and Tangle</b><br><br>However, it also suggests that an author doesn't necessarily understand the true effect or import of their own tale.<br><br>My book is not necessarily what I intended. Ironically, it might be no more than what I realised.<br><br>Still, a tale requires a listener, just as much as a speaker, so we don't know its meaning, until we know how it has been heard.<br><br>A tale, therefore, is constructed by both author and reader.<br><br>What's important, too, is how well the tale is told. Its appeal is in the telling. It doesn't have to be perfect, as long as it is fit for purpose or is entertaining. <br><br>Being a tale, it's also delivered in parts. We might enjoy some parts more than others:<br><br><i>"Tales are like tarts, that may be ugly on the face of 'em and yet have a worthwhile end."</i><br><br><b>Innocence and Experience</b><br><br>For all the bawdy humour, the novel deals incidentally, at least, with serious issues.<br><br>At heart, it's a tale of innocence and experience.<br><br>The virgin poet Ebenezer Cooke and his twin sister, Anna, are the innocents. Their former tutor and friend, Henry Burlingame III, is the experienced one.<br><br>There is a creative tension between the three, although Henry is the primary source of it: like the author himself, <i>"he makes game of my innocence".</i> <br><br>Needless to say, the game encompasses twins, coupling and couplets! Entwining, swiving and rhyming abounds! And so it should! Pretty or not, it makes no claim to virtue.<br><br><b>Preachment and Practice</b><br><br>Of course, innocence comes before experience. Hence, innocence is associated with virginity; the loss of it with the fall and subsequent worldly experience:<br><br><i>" 'Twas carnal knowledge, knowledge of the flesh, that caused man's fall."</i><br><br>In Henry's case, it also motivated and drove his engagement with the world:<br><br><i>"Yet anon I lost [my virginity], and so committed me to the world; 'twas then I vowed, since I was fallen from grace, I would worship the Serpent that betrayed me, and ere I died would know the taste of every fruit the garden grows!"</i><br><br><b>Sneak and Subterfuge</b><br><br>So begins Ebenezer's quest to learn about the (new) world (of Maryland), if not necessarily lose his virginity.<br><br>Still, everywhere he goes in this not quite Virgin Maryland, he encounters Henry in his various (dis-)guises:<br><br><i>"He loves the world, and comprehends it at first glance - sometimes even sight unseen - yet his love is flavoured with a similar contempt, from the selfsame cause, which leads him to make game of what he loves."</i><br><br><b>Sundry Trials and Impostures</b><br><br>Henry, who ironically has <i>"nor wealth, nor place, nor even parentage",</i> is far more relaxed with the world. He doesn't strive to understand it in its totality. He seeks only to understand himself within it:<br><br><i>"One must needs make and seize his soul, and then cleave fast to't, or go babbling in the corner; one must choose his gods and devils on the run, quill his own name upon the universe, and declare, " 'Tis I, and the world stands such-a-way!" One must assert, assert, assert, or go screaming mad."</i><br><br>Henry confronts real life every moment of the day, often masked or impersonating an other:<br><br><i>"I know you not from one hour to the next...The world's a happy climate for imposture."</i><br><br><b>Factions and Intrigues</b><br><br>Personality is fluid and fragmented. Nothing is whole. Each of us has a <i>"driven and fragmented spirit."</i> We have to reinvent or rediscover ourselves step by step on the journey through life. Henry advises Ebenezer:<br><br><i>"You must embrace your Self as Poet and Virgin, regardless, or discard it for something better. In either case don't seek whole understanding - the search were fruitless, and there is no time for't."</i><br><br>Ostensibly, the novel is the tale of Ebenezer's education. However, his rival is equally educated over the course of the novel, for all his worldliness and playfulness.<br><br><b>Idlers and Ne'er-Do-Wells</b><br><br>Like most in the American colonies, Henry is an orphan. The absence of a father means the absence of a father figure, and therefore a source of authority.<br><br>Just as orphans might lack a heritage, some lack a moral compass. These are the men who colonised the New World:<br><br><i>"The plain fact is, the greatest part are castaways: rebels, failures, jailbirds and adventurers. Cast such seed on such soil, and 'twere fond to seek a crop of dons and courtiers..There is a freedom there that's both a blessing and a curse, for't means both liberty and lawlessness. 'Tis more than just political and religious liberty - they come and go from one year to the next. 'Tis philosophic liberty I speak of, that comes from want of history. It throws one on his own resources, that freedom - makes every man an orphan like myself and can as well demoralise as elevate."</i><br><br><b>Morals and Metaphysics</b><br><br>America's origins are therefore both de-moralised and demoralised.<br><br>When Ebenezer arrives in Maryland, it is fast going from sot-weed (tobacco) to pot (well, opium, actually).<br><br>Ebenezer's poetry, his culture, his civilisation is no solution. It's too removed from reality:<br><br><i>"Literature...availed him not, for though it afforded one a certain sophistication about life and a release from one's single mortal destiny, it did not, except accidentally, afford solutions to practical problems."</i><br><br><b>Shifting and Confounded</b><br><br>For all Man's love of Reason, there is no order or logic in Life. History too is a fabrication, ours:<br><br><i>"We all invent our pasts, more or less, as we go along, at the dictates of Whim and Interest; the happenings of former times are a clay in the present moment that will-we, nill-we, the lot of us must sculpt. Thus Being does make Positivists of us all. Moreover, this Clio was already a scarred and crafty trollop when the Author found her."</i><br><br><b>Toss and Tempest</b><br><br>Ultimately, Life is a tempest, that tosses us around on the waters: <i>"This thing we call civilisation...'tis a bumboat-load o' judges, dons and poets, on a dark and vasty main o'erwracked with storms".</i> <br><br>Life is beyond our control, and that of our factors and agents. We might be a character on its stage, but it tells its own tale, fearless of outcome or coincidence or absurdity We don't write Life; it writes us:<br><br><i>"Life is a shameless playwright."</i><br><br>But so were Rabelais and Shakespeare, and so is Barth. All three of them have writ large about Life for our reading pleasure.<br><br>Their subject matter is the stuff of life, drawn both dramatic and comic, tragic and farcical. In Barth's twinned words (many of which I've used for my sub-headings), this novel contains within: fops and fools, love and candor, lust and pride, trysts and secret meetings, hypocrisy and lewd delight, gasps and titters.<br><br>Some might equal these tales and their telling, but none are better. At least, none that come to mind.<br><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1428446017i/14425171._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="250" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Detail of Back Cover and Spine Illustration by Owen Wood<br><br><br><br><b>ADDED EXTRAS:<br>[Couplets and Eulogies]</b><br><br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br><br><b>I Shiver at the Memory <br>(Of Joan Toast)<br>[In the Words of John Barth]</b><br><br>'Twas in my chamber<br>I saw her last,<br>As pink and naked<br>As a lover's dream.<br>You'd not believe <br>How fine her fair skin feels,<br>Or how tight and sprightly<br>Is her whole small body.<br>How could I forget<br>The fat of her little buttocks<br>O'ertop the hard young muscle?<br>Or the softness of her breasts<br>That gently flattened<br>When she lay supine,<br>But hung like apples of Heav'n<br>When she bent o'er me?<br>I shiver at the memory. <br><br><br><b>The Hudibrastic Virgin Falls for a Scarlet Harlot</b><br><br>On virgin shelf,<br>Eb found himself.<br>With wealth nigh nought,<br>Five guineas short,<br>His night ended,<br>Unbefriended<br>By Mistress Joan,<br>In bed alone.<br>Though he dreams of<br>A new found love,<br>We must deduct,<br>He's still un<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[plucked.<br><br> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br><br><b>The Clucky Cuckold's Wife</b><br><br>Come, listen<br>To some stories,<br>Writ down in rhymes<br>From olden times.<br>In these verses,<br>Bawdy, but old,<br>Poet versus <br>Rustic cuckold,<br>Who's too busy<br>To prize the treats<br>Or plough the field<br>Between his sheets. <br>While farmer's off<br>On his tractor.<br>What poets do<br>Does attract her.<br>No farmer he,<br>The poet comes<br>With virile seed<br>And furrowed brow.<br>When soon she begs,<br>He's ready now<br>To fertilise<br>Her monthly eggs.<br>Although he writes<br>What's in his mind,<br>His tale is told<br>Between her legs.<br><br><br><b>Folio Society [Turn the Page]</b><br><br>If recto can't<br>Accomplish,<br>Then verso might<br>Astonish.<br><br><br><b>The Original Poem "The Sotweed Factor"<br>By Ebenezer Cook</b><br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://pages.uoregon.edu/rbear/sotweed.htm">http://pages.uoregon.edu/rbear/sotwee...</a><br><br><br><b>A Bio of the Real Ebenezer Cook</b><br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://newfoundpress.utk.edu//pubs/lemay/part_two_cook.pdf">http://newfoundpress.utk.edu//pubs/le...</a><br><br> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]>     <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1240533235" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1240533235" />
    <a title="Mona" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7020601-mona"><img alt="Mona" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1392557851p2/7020601.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1240533235?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mona" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mona" href="/user/show/7020601-mona">Mona</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/25254521-the-sot-weed-factor">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7020601-mona?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1240533235" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17495112918464812776"><b>Boisterous, Hilarious, Satirical, Epic Frolic Set in Seventeenth Century London and Colonial Maryland</b><br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1428434108i/14422318.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>A Goodreads buddy described this book as "a rollicking tale". Good description.<br/><br/>Don't expect brevity or logic here.<br/><br/>I already knew Barth was a formidable and unique writer, since I'd read and loved <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12885.Lost_in_the_Funhouse' title='Lost in the Funhouse by John Barth' rel='nofollow noopener'>Lost in the Funhouse</a> a long time ago.<br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24835.The_Sot_Weed_Factor' title='The Sot-Weed Factor by John Barth' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Sot-Weed Factor</a> is an entirely different type of novel. So Barth, like the most brilliant writers, is extremely versatile and has proven he can write well in comp</span>
  <span id="freeText17495112918464812776" style="display:none"><b>Boisterous, Hilarious, Satirical, Epic Frolic Set in Seventeenth Century London and Colonial Maryland</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1428434108i/14422318.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>A Goodreads buddy described this book as "a rollicking tale". Good description.<br><br>Don't expect brevity or logic here.<br><br>I already knew Barth was a formidable and unique writer, since I'd read and loved <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12885.Lost_in_the_Funhouse" title="Lost in the Funhouse by John Barth" rel="nofollow noopener">Lost in the Funhouse</a> a long time ago.<br><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24835.The_Sot_Weed_Factor" title="The Sot-Weed Factor by John Barth" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sot-Weed Factor</a> is an entirely different type of novel. So Barth, like the most brilliant writers, is extremely versatile and has proven he can write well in completely different styles. <br><br>Barth's lengthy novel, written with very authentic dialogue (and, in the texts-within-the texts, written style) of the times, tracks the innumerable adventures of one Ebenezer Cooke, who was born in England in 1666 and died in Maryland in 1732 (or at least that's the arc of the novel). Our hero is based on a real poet, Ebenezer Cook (sometimes spelled Cooke) who lived around the same time and wrote a biting satirical poem scourging life in Maryland entitled "The Sot-Weed Factor". (In the idiom of that time, a sot-weed factor is a tobacco dealer). Little is known of the real Ebenezer Cook. This real poem and poet are obviously the source of Barth's title and his main character. The poem is also the source of certain basic plot elements. But that's like saying a vegetable is the source of a culinary masterpiece or a simple tune is the source of a complex jazz piece. Barth, our chef/jazzman (expressing himself through his characters) creates complex riffs on the original undreamed of by the real Cook.<br><br>Ebenezer Cooke is a genuine horse's ass at the beginning of the novel. He's pompous, pretentious, and hoarding his innocence (both of life and of sex) like it's gold (although at least in the beginning of the book, he's quite reserved). Only later on does he start trumpeting his virtues. He's about to flunk out of Cambridge, since he's started dabbling with writing verse . Ebenezer is also paralyzed by a lack of direction. His problem is that he finds every path in life equally appealing and cannot decide between them.<br><br>In the book's opening paragraph, Barth describes Ebenezer thus:<br><br><i>"IN THE LAST YEARS OF THE SEVENTEENTH CENTURY THERE WAS TO BE found among the fops and fools of the London coffee-houses one rangy, gangling flitch called Ebenezer Cooke, more ambitious than talented, and yet more talented than prudent, who, like his friends-in-folly, all of whom were supposed to be educating at Oxford or Cambridge, had found the sound of Mother English more fun to game with than her sense to labor over, and so rather than applying himself to the pains of scholarship, had learned the knack of versifying, and ground out quires of couplets after the fashion of the day, afroth with Joves and Jupiters, aclang with jarring rhymes, and string-taut with similes stretched to the snapping-point"</i><br><br>Ebenezer gets a surprise visit in his Cambridge rooms from his former tutor, Henry Burlingame. Burlingame had disappeared for no apparent reason after teaching Ebenezer and his twin sister, Anna, at the estate of their father, Andrew, in St. Giles in the Fields. Burlingame was a fantastic teacher and curious about all subjects and the world in general. Ebenezer's indecision about his life's path is in part a result of Burlingame's eclectic tutelage, that made all life paths seem equally desirable.<br><br>Burlingame, hearing that Ebenezer is about to be kicked out of Cambridge, explains his disappearance (Andrew Cooke had presumed that Burlingame had designs on Anna, and fired him on the spot). He also proposes a business venture: that Ebenezer should join him in London in a tutoring business. <br><br><i>"Unhappy day!" laughed Ebenezer. "I've no skill in any craft or trade<br>whatever. I cannot even play Flow My Tears on the guitar. I can do nothing/'<br>"Then 'tis plain you'll be a teacher, like myself."<br>"'Sheart! Twould be the blind leading the blind!"<br>"Aye,* smiled Burlingame. "Who better grasps the trials of sightlessness<br>than he whose eyes are gone?"<br>"But what teach? I know something of many things, and enough of<br>naught."<br>"I'faith, then the field is open, and you may graze where you list."<br>"Teach a thing I know naught of?" exclaimed Ebenezer.<br>"And raise thy fee for't," replied Burlingame.</i><br><br>Ebenezer decides he's no teacher, and goes home to face his father. Andrew Cooke decrees that Ebenezer must apprentice as a clerk for a time in London and then sail to Maryland to oversee Andrew Cooke's estate there, Malden.<br><br>Ebenezer has no talent for or interest in, clerking either:<br><br> <i> "He would begin to add a column of totally meaningless figures and realize five minutes later that he'd been staring at<br>a wen on the neck of the boy in front of him, or rehearsing in his mind a<br>real or imaginary conversation between himself and Burlingame, or drawing<br>mazes on a bit of scratch-paper. For the same reason, though he had<br>by no means the troublemaker's temperament, his untamable fancy more<br>than once led him to be charged with irresponsibility: one day, for example, scarcely conscious of what he was about, he involved himself entirely in a game with a small black ant that had wandered across the page."</i><br><br>He spends several years in London and "fares unspectacularly" in Barth's words.<br><br>He then gets himself into more trouble (he seems to have a knack for getting into trouble and dragging everyone around him into it). He falls in love with a whore, the humorously named Joan Toast, and refuses to pay John McEvoy, her pimp and lover, on the grounds that he cannot bring himself to pay for a woman he has fallen in love with.<br>McEvoy then mails a letter to Andrew declaring that Ebenezer has been spending time with whores, not advancing in his job, etc. As a result, Andrew sends Ebenezer to Maryland.<br><br>Before Ebenezer leaves London, he dresses up in his best (and humorously depicted) finery and pays a call on Lord Baltimore, persuading him to make him Poet Laureate of Maryland.<br><br>Ebenezer commences to have many misadventures, including multiple captures by pirates, several near drownings, being robbed of all his worldly goods (several times), being captured by Indians, etc. He also commits a petty theft of his own after nearly getting in a duel with a stationery/book seller who drives him crazy by offering him too many notebook options (slim cardboard quarto with unlined paper, thick leather quarto with lined paper, etc.) Ebenezer hates options because, since they are all equally attractive to him, he can never choose between them. He encounters various shady characters (such as the aptly named Captains Slye and Scurry), shysters, quacks, shady lawyers, golden-hearted whores and disreputable women, pirates, thieves, spies, traitors, imposters, rebellious Natives and slaves, Indian kings, etc. But others often take Eben himself for a madman.<br><br>The novel is a comedy of errors (albeit with a somewhat sobering postscript).<br><br>The themes of trickster, criminals, and especially disguises and mistaken and faked identities pervade the entire book (as well as that of twins). We also have changes in social and cultural identities occurring on a regular basis (certain characters switch between being Indians and being English colonists; others morph from being servants to being gentlemen (and vice versa). <br><br>Burlingame disappears and reappears at regular intervals in both England and Maryland. He assumes various disguises and identities (many of which don't become obvious until later in the book). He's a master trickster. He's also clearly a spy, although for whom is not clear until the novel's end. Orphaned Burlingame is also obsessed with finding his parentage and origins. Ebenezer helps with this task.<br><br>The Laureateship causes multiple problems. It engenders numerous "Ebenezer Cooke" impersonators, who of course, also cause Ebenezer trouble.<br><br>Also, coincidences abound (many of which strain credulity, but credulity is not the point here). Or as Barth puts it:<br><br><i>” Lest it be objected by a certain stodgy variety of squint-minded antiquarians that he has in this lengthy history played more fast and loose with Clio, the chronicler's muse, than ever Captain John Smith dared, the Author here posits in advance, by way of surety, three blue-chip replies arranged in order of decreasing relevancy. In the first place be it remembered, as Burlingame himself observed, that we all invent our pasts, more or less, as we go along, at the dictates of Whim and Interest; the happenings of former times are a clay in the present moment that will-we, nill-we, the lot of us must sculpt... Moreover, this Clio was already a scarred and crafty trollop when the Author found her; it wants a nice-honed casuist, with her sort, to separate seducer from seduced. But if, despite all, he is convicted at the Public Bar of having<br>forced what slender virtue the strumpet may make claim to, then the Author<br>joins with pleasure the most engaging company imaginable, his fellow fornicators, whose ranks include the noblest in poetry, prose, and politics"</i><br><br>Ebenezer runs into many of the same people he met in London (or who showed up in others' tales) again and again in Maryland. Many of them turn out to have (previously unknown) connections to others in Ebenezer's life. Ebenezer also encounters new characters in Maryland , such as Mary Mungommery "The Travelling Whore of Dorchester" and Harvey Russecks, a trapper. <br><br>There are a couple of key players who (possibly---we are never certain) never actually appear in person in the novel. We are not really sure if they exist or not. <br>The themes of shifting and uncertain identities were also explored by Barth in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12885.Lost_in_the_Funhouse" title="Lost in the Funhouse by John Barth" rel="nofollow noopener">Lost in the Funhouse</a> <br><br>There's plenty of meta here. There are innumerable (and very entertaining) tales within tales. In fact, it seems like almost every person Ebenezer encounters has a yarn to tell. There are also a couple of manuscripts (memoirs) within the novel, not to mention Ebenezer's poetry. Maybe that's why Barth insists this is "postmodern", although I really don't see that it is. <br><br>Ebenezer does evolve through the novel. He goes from being a terrible poet to a pretty good one. He also realizes that the innocence he's set such store on is not worth as much as he thought. Innocence (and its loss) is also a theme throughout the book.<br><br>This is a kind of picaresque novel crossed with a Bildungsroman. And it's remiscent of novels of that time period, such as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/76527.The_Life_and_Opinions_of_Tristram_Shandy__Gentleman" title="The Life and Opinions of Tristram Shandy, Gentleman by Laurence Sterne" rel="nofollow noopener">The Life and Opinions of Tristram Shandy, Gentleman</a>. <br><br>Anyway, this novel is hilarious and great fun to read, although I do admit it seemed a bit long (although its length is probably appropriate for its epic sweep).<br><br>Kevin Pariseau does an absolutely masterful job of reading the audio and voicing the different characters. I read along in the Kindle book and the PDF version, both of which are available free online, since I think this novel might now be in the public domain.<br><br>Here's a link to the original poem: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://pages.uoregon.edu/rbear/sotweed.htm#N_38_">http://pages.uoregon.edu/rbear/sotwee...</a><br><br>Thanks to Ian Pagan-Szary for this link. Also thanks to Ian for suggesting we do a "buddy read" on this, which inspired me to tackle this long book sooner rather than later.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17495112918464812776" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_481885651" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/481885651" />
    <a title="Mariel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/647547-mariel"><img alt="Mariel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451321467p2/647547.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/481885651?book_show_action=true">Dec 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mariel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mariel" href="/user/show/647547-mariel">Mariel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">for want of a name the shoe was lost </span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Mariel by: </span>
      Shananabananafofana
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer481885651" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5749693481438198403"><blockquote>We sit here on a blind rock careening through space; we are all of us rushing headlong to the grave. Think you the worms will care, when anon they make a meal of you, whether you spent your moment sighing wigless in your chamber, or sacked the golden tombs of Montezuma? Lookee, the day’s nigh spent; ’tis gone careering into time forever… We are dying men: i’faith, there’s time for naught but bold resolves!</blockquote><br/><br/>It kills me when I go to the movies and I'm sitting next to some little kid that has to stu</span>
  <span id="freeText5749693481438198403" style="display:none"><blockquote>We sit here on a blind rock careening through space; we are all of us rushing headlong to the grave. Think you the worms will care, when anon they make a meal of you, whether you spent your moment sighing wigless in your chamber, or sacked the golden tombs of Montezuma? Lookee, the day’s nigh spent; ’tis gone careering into time forever… We are dying men: i’faith, there’s time for naught but bold resolves!</blockquote><br /><br />It kills me when I go to the movies and I'm sitting next to some little kid that has to study my reaction to the story for their cue to laugh or whatever reaction is appropriate. I'll start to look at the kid to see if they are doing what I'm doing. I'll get distracted looking at the adult they are with and wondering what relationship dynamic they share that they aren't studying what they are doing. Why choose me? Then there's that feeling when someone is watching you and you feel pressured to give them that big laugh. They'll repeat the joke if you didn't laugh hard enough. Didn't you get it? It's hilarious! These feelings aren't conducive for that blissful wrapped up in a story feeling that I can't get enough. I don't want to be prodded into laughing over endless dick and fart jokes. I want to roll over and go to sleep and not wake up with a vegetable poking me into a further vegetated state.<br /><br />I can't remember the last time that I really laughed. I mean a full on bellyaching sidesplitting laugh. It may be me. My goodreads friends list seem to love this one to pieces. The whole thing and the ensuing of hilarity. If it were a film I'd expect the sound bites to read something like "The thrill ride of the summer!" and "I laughed until I cried!" Reviews all over the internet suggest it is a book to be loved. Ribald, raunchy, corset poppin', romps, sweaty, sheets, rolling, rollicking, over the side, walk the plank, tied up, buttered up, tarred, feathered, skinned, scalped, pokered, buggered, roped, raped, underneath, hole, fit it, in, penis, vagina, poop. Blah blah blah. It was okay enough, at first. I started out wondering why this blank faced young man was looking at everyone else in the movie theatre for his cue to look like he felt about anything. I didn't really find it hilarious but I wasn't desperately bored, yet. Have you ever read series fiction? And you have to get through a lot of boring bits that reiterate information from previous volumes? One that I used to read before I got bored with it was Michelle Sagara's Elantra series (an excellent example, if you are familiar with it, because I doubt it'll ever end, or at least it seemed that way when I gave up). Every damned time I had to sit through scenes of the lead character being late to everything. She's always late! Look, she's a klutz! Scene after scene of this. In case you forgot (which I hadn't). The Sot-Weed Factor is one book. I got really damned bored reading about stuff that just happened as if I had read about it years ago in an earlier volume when I hadn't because it was the same book that I was reading at that time (I read it while waiting all day and into night for emergency car service. If I had had ANY other book with me I would have given up). I guess it is probably a satire of serial series too? You know how back in the day (or now, if it is the awful The Hobbit film(s)) they would stretch out stories beyond (en)durability to milk out the most money? Yeah. I felt like I was rereading books that I read in my teen series or early twenties. I probably would be less moany about this one if I wasn't writing a review of it. It's the energy it takes to have a reaction to something and finding out you didn't really care that much. I don't feel like writing reviews of Tristam Shandy or any of the J.P. Donleavy books. I'm really reminded of Philip from Of Human Bondage. It's a "history" of a life, same as that book, and the hero is a cypher for a parade of other people's stories. I liked Megha's nicer way of seeing it that it was an echo of the storytelling nature of lives past. To me it felt like going from soap box to soap box to espouse whatever the author felt like talking about, and not much of a life for the hero. He's always looking to everyone else and then he writes about it. He would probably hold a quill to his lip and dream dreamy dreams about love and life and not understand any of it because he never lived any of it. <br /><br />Ebeneezer depressed me. I just saw him as some dick waving asshole confronting the prostitute Joan with his "virtue". It is supposed to be like this! Tell me what it is supposed to be like, somebody. Somebody live up to something so I don't have to get in the dirt. The poor girl on the ship that he is disappointed was not a virgin when the other guy rapes her before he had meant to rape her himself. (Like Phillip's entitlement to a hot girl despite his bum leg. It amazes me he's not seen as a dick for wanting a hot girl instead of the supposedly ugly girls who loved him but Mildred was evil because she wanted a hot guy instead of him.) I don't know. This is innocence? I saw a monster face hiding behind not having one. He wonders about his own halted desires and stilted virtues. Sure, he notices it to talk about it. Talking a lot and making claims on the future and meaning isn't innocence to me. Of course, the fictional Ebeneezer Cooke (there was a real one) likes to run around and call himself a poet (I grew so tired of how often Barth refers to him as "the poet" as a name). Names aren't art. Innocence would be living in spite of it and art would be making something in spite of it, or just having something else to live in along with it. Some place to go. What was all of these hijinks and antics and adventures and scenes scenes scenes saying? No, don't look at the face of the person next to you, Mariel. You didn't like it that much.<br /><br />His twin sister moves between serenity of listening to her father, or her brother, or their tutor Burlingame. Another no expression. She has Burlingame's baby and their neighbors believe it is her brother's child. They have to live with faces coming up with conclusions. <br /><br />I was interested from time to time in the horrors of indentured servitude. I am fascinated by the corruption and cruelty enacted on fellow men in "contracts" (not feeding people to "save money"). But then I never thought that colonization was a pure thing without warts on the ass. I sort of cared about Mary and the Inn-keeper's daughter. They tried to live anyway (mirroring the wet nurse who fell into trouble with a man because another authority male figure kept her locked up from life. So they took lives for their own). I liked that Joan could live as herself and like sex anyway, even as Ebeneezer hearing the story of the rape by trickery from her uncle (after her first bleeding) tells her that she should feel the horror and shame. I don't care about what anyone says anyone else should be feeling. There wasn't anyone alive then who didn't have to live with something. I guess I don't give any kind of damn about satirizing other novels. If they aren't living their life that says a lot more about them than it does about their teachers or parents or that life wasn't handed to them on a clean silver platter. I was just happy to finish it and never have to read it again.<br /><br />At least the prostitutes were diseased. It is refreshing to read about prostitutes in novels that aren't just dream girls for some man, no matter what Ebeneezer thought. It sucks and they do get haggard and have sexually transmitted diseases (Thomas Pynchon in Inherent Vice I am talking to you). Of course, I don't need an enormous book to tell me that either. I would have been better off reading a book about the colony of Maryland and imagining stories out of that myself then trying to squint and imagine history and stories out of why aren't you laughing? I just made another sex joke.<br /><br />P.s. I read The Sound and the Fury that is an intensely style focused novel obsessed with virginity after reading this intensely stylized novel about a man obsessed with his own virginity. Uh oh. Is this a sign of my wet blanket existence going further into my future? I never find Kristen's penis drawings funny. She thought I thought they were obscene and that's why I disliked them. I just didn't feel anything at all. Pubic hair in the pictures was too intimate for me, though. I can imagine it getting into food, for some reason. Burlingame's suggestion for vegetables and penis will ruin this vegetarian's appetite. I just don't find it funny. I feel nothing. I don't want to think about not feeling anything like I'm lacking. I just don't have the same sense of humor. Maybe this book is for everyone else and I'm on another ship going to another colony where they scalp Princess Tiger Lilly.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5749693481438198403" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1901253760?book_show_action=true">Sep 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward" href="/user/show/12933866-edward">Edward</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780586092163" href="/book/show/1222743.The_Sot_Weed_Factor">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=2017">2017</a>
          </div>
